<span id="freeTextContainer11156333617407579201">When I first read this book many years ago, I was shocked by the information that I was exposed to. There were very many radical ideas being promulgated by the author. More than a few of those ideas challenged the underpinnings of my worldview. It was a challenge to Western consensus reality. I did not accept the author's assertions on face value. Some of the things he was suggesting that were true, if he was right, would mean that many beliefs I held were based on inaccurate and/or incomplete i</span>
  <span id="freeText11156333617407579201" style="display:none">When I first read this book many years ago, I was shocked by the information that I was exposed to. There were very many radical ideas being promulgated by the author. More than a few of those ideas challenged the underpinnings of my worldview. It was a challenge to Western consensus reality. I did not accept the author's assertions on face value. Some of the things he was suggesting that were true, if he was right, would mean that many beliefs I held were based on inaccurate and/or incomplete information; poorly understood or misunderstood concepts; and outright untruths. How could this be? I set out to answer that question and ascertain (as well as I could) the veracity or otherwise of the claims in the book. About thirty years later I am still working on it. But I found very quickly that there was a vast, solid foundation of Truth that made up the ground upon which Idries Shah stood. If you haven't read this work and are unfamiliar with it, then please buy it and read it without delay. It could easily be the most important book you will ever read. And some knowledge that you will get from it might very well save your life.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5534802-gwen?shelf=islam-related">islam-related</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer316195760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15791012641887056972">I was really excited to read this book when I first got it. Quite a few people that I know talk about Indries Shah and his writing. The way the book is written wasn't really all that interesting to me and eventually I became bored. I don't want to be offensive here. There are wonderful parts of this book, but there are also a good number of parts in this book where my eyes glazed over a little bit (especially the many parts where the numerical values and many many definitions of words were laid </span>
  <span id="freeText15791012641887056972" style="display:none">I was really excited to read this book when I first got it. Quite a few people that I know talk about Indries Shah and his writing. The way the book is written wasn't really all that interesting to me and eventually I became bored. I don't want to be offensive here. There are wonderful parts of this book, but there are also a good number of parts in this book where my eyes glazed over a little bit (especially the many parts where the numerical values and many many definitions of words were laid out). The work didn't feel very alive to me, and I felt like I was being preached to. Neither of which is something I tend to enjoy in a book. I love the first couple of chapters, but as they go on I began skipping them one after the other. If you want a very detailed book about Sufism, this is it. I can definitely see how many people would love this book, it just wasn't my cup of tea. This is a great work on Sufism, it just isn't that approachable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15791012641887056972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3062801674" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3062801674" />
    <a title="Akshunya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26807831-akshunya"><img alt="Akshunya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551460764p2/26807831.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3062801674?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Akshunya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Akshunya" href="/user/show/26807831-akshunya">Akshunya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25407300-the-sufis">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3062801674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5931688468623542434">The Sufis is a very comprehensive 410 pages book which explains in great detail, the historical background, cultural impact and spiritual discipline of Sufism, including the lives of great Sufi masters like Omar Khayyam and Jalaluddin Rumi to name a few.<br/><br/>I came across the writer of this book, 'Idries Shah', while watching a very interesting BBC documentary, 'DreamWalkers'. <br/>(Available on YouTube: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1v0oGh7mTV0'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1v0oG...</a>)<br/>And then i got to know more about him from an audio recording</span>
  <span id="freeText5931688468623542434" style="display:none">The Sufis is a very comprehensive 410 pages book which explains in great detail, the historical background, cultural impact and spiritual discipline of Sufism, including the lives of great Sufi masters like Omar Khayyam and Jalaluddin Rumi to name a few.<br /><br />I came across the writer of this book, 'Idries Shah', while watching a very interesting BBC documentary, 'DreamWalkers'. <br />(Available on YouTube: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1v0oGh7mTV0">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1v0oG...</a>)<br />And then i got to know more about him from an audio recording of a Nobel Prize winning Author 'Dorris Lessing'.<br />(Available on YouTube: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bI5cdTptJK0">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bI5cd...</a>)<br />I was quite fascinated by his ideas and how he presented those, that i finally decided to read his most read book, which is 'The Sufis'.<br /><br />Before i go into what i really liked in the book, i would like to mention a few things which did not go that well as far as the experience of reading it is concerned.<br /><br />First of all the characters. I mean, i know it is a non fiction book, but if you are not that familiar with the names of the people he talks about, it will be difficult to create an image of those people and relate to them. For example, whereas it was quick and easy to read about Rumi and Khayyam (well known figures, even outside Sufi circles), it wasn't, when he talks about people like Ibn el-Arabi or Attar, it slows down the reading considerably. And the real problem is that there are too many people he talks about, and at one point it all starts getting mixed up.<br /><br />Then the part which i had a tough time reading, was 'Mysteries in the West'. It talks about places and things unheard of (at least for me), in unusual landscapes, with technical details of the Sufi Practices and i must admit that i skipped a couple of chapters, because i was not enjoying it at all.<br /><br />Moving on to the parts which i really liked, i will start with the treasure of insights that this book holds. I would like to share a few here:<br /><br />• "It is too often taken for granted that a person must either be a believer or an unbeliever, or perhaps an agnostic. If a believer, he will expect to be offered a faith or a system which seems to him to fulfil what he thinks are his needs. Few people tell him that he may not understand what his needs are."<br /><br />• "The “good” man is, of course, the man who wants for others what he wants for himself. The single problem of this is that what he wants is often the last thing which he needs.’"<br /><br />• "‘If you will not reprove yourself,’ Saadi says, ‘you will not welcome reproof from another.’"<br /><br />• Anchorites, who are nothing more than professional obsessives, have given the impression that the desert or mountains are the places where the mystic must spend his whole life. They have mistaken a thread for the whole carpet.<br /><br />And there are so many of these.<br /><br />The book consists of so many fables, short stories and jokes, which communicate things on a different level, where simple descriptive explanations fail.<br /><br />We are also made familiar to the impact, which Sufi way of life has, all over the world (Including India), which is somewhat amazing to know that how widespread that impact is, and on things and people which we generally do not associate with Sufism.<br /><br />A very important aspect which is talked about in this book, which can be understood as the underlying theme of the book and which also hits hard to our egos, is that:<br />Humanity is Asleep and concerned with what is useless, only to repent at the end, for the lost opportunity which was given to it as this Gift called Life. There is a reality beyond what we are able to perceive, and it needs special effort and conditions to enable us to see that reality. It is slightly demotivating to go through it, and realize how limited our lives are, or worse, how limited we ourselves have made our lives.<br /><br />Overall it was worth reading and opened up so many dimensions, which we dont come across in our day to day lives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5931688468623542434" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3382885213" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3382885213" />
    <a title="Mike Macdonald" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/116634480-mike-macdonald"><img alt="Mike Macdonald" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3382885213?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike Macdonald" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike Macdonald" href="/user/show/116634480-mike-macdonald">Mike Macdonald</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3382885213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16875651210849004228">I am renewing my acquaintance with this volume of basic information about the Sufi phenomenon. Idries Shah spent his life re-educating the world about this vital element of culture and possible enlightenment. He gathered the most relevant information, illustrative teaching stories and planted the influence of this teaching in a manner that would speak to the modern world mindset, while maintaining the spirit of this ancient tradition.<br/><br/>I find “The Sufis” has a fresh resonance that I perhaps didn’t</span>
  <span id="freeText16875651210849004228" style="display:none">I am renewing my acquaintance with this volume of basic information about the Sufi phenomenon. Idries Shah spent his life re-educating the world about this vital element of culture and possible enlightenment. He gathered the most relevant information, illustrative teaching stories and planted the influence of this teaching in a manner that would speak to the modern world mindset, while maintaining the spirit of this ancient tradition.<br /><br />I find “The Sufis” has a fresh resonance that I perhaps didn’t sense, reading it some time ago. Having become familiar with many of Shah’s books over the past decades has given me a taste of the evolution and completeness of his presentation. Let me mention a few of the impressions that strike me this time around. The opening story of the exiled islanders seems to be a parable of our condition, our psychological predicament, and the obstacles we face in becoming a “boat builder” or “swimmers” —opening possibilities for a return to our true nature and destiny.<br /><br />One fascinating aspect of the book is a periodic description of one key to necessarily hidden teachings with multiple meanings, a secret language based on semitic consonantal roots, most often used by Sufi poets. When I first encountered the book, I skipped over these extensive explanations of coded words. Now I begin to see them as foundational yet flexible structures that much of Shah’s later work will build upon.<br /><br />My favorite chapter is a primer on the wise-fool teacher, Mulla Nasrudin, whose storied shenanigans I have taken to heart over the last four decades. Shah has published four volumes from his collections of these tales, often told and retold in tea houses of the Middle East and Asia. If you taste a few and get hooked, then I recommend this chapter as reminder and guide to the deeper layers of meaning and experience that can possibly be found in these humorously entertaining vehicles of enlightenment.<br /><br />I’ll mention a few more strands of information, I notice woven into the fabric of the of this work — the importance of exemplars in this tradition, with chapters on classical figures, including prominent thinkers from the west, who absorbed and infused our culture with seeds and centers of this higher human learning capacity. The obstacles in simply approaching the “people of the way”, and the basic need to set aside ones assumptions in order to begin to “learn how to learn.” The inability of most narrow minded scholars to learn within the needed experiential parameters of a different realm of knowledge. The vital role of teaching stories and analogical examples. Flexibility of methods and atmosphere fitted to local culture and mindset. Right time, place and people. <br /><br />I hope this review may wet a few appetites. One could also begin with one of Shah’s collections of stories — like “The Magic Monastery” or “Caravan of Dreams.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16875651210849004228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_968484040" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/968484040" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6016605-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516381800p2/6016605.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/968484040?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/6016605-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer968484040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11594035583905079421">I checked out <i>The Sufis</i> by Idries Shah on a whim. I ran across it while looking for his English translations of the “Tales of Nasrudin”.<br/><br/>I’ve long been fascinated by the Sufi. The works of Rumi and Omar Khayyám, of Hafiz, Ibn el-Arabi, Ghazali, and Averroës, all had powerful effects on me when I first read them, and each time I reread them. The Sufi exerted a powerful and largely unappreciated influence over the course of Western culture and history.<br/><br/>Mr. Shah is an esteemed author and a Sufi. I th</span>
  <span id="freeText11594035583905079421" style="display:none">I checked out <i>The Sufis</i> by Idries Shah on a whim. I ran across it while looking for his English translations of the “Tales of Nasrudin”.<br /><br />I’ve long been fascinated by the Sufi. The works of Rumi and Omar Khayyám, of Hafiz, Ibn el-Arabi, Ghazali, and Averroës, all had powerful effects on me when I first read them, and each time I reread them. The Sufi exerted a powerful and largely unappreciated influence over the course of Western culture and history.<br /><br />Mr. Shah is an esteemed author and a Sufi. I thought this book would be a good history and insight into their beliefs and practices.<br /><br />It’s tempting to use this review to analyze and critique the beliefs and claims of the Sufi. But the real question isn’t whether or not I agree, or even sympathize, with Sufi philosophy and practice.<br /><br />The real question is whether or not this book successfully conveys an understanding of the Sufi to a non-Sufi reader. And the answer is:<br /><br />Yes?<br /><br />My answer to this question can’t be unqualified.<br /><br />There’s benefit in reading this book. It offers a tremendous amount of information about the Sufi from someone who is, himself, a Sufi. At the time this work was published, such a perspective didn’t properly exist in the Western literature on the subject. That alone makes this work important.<br /><br />In truth, the greatest benefit I find in the book is the unique Sufi / Persian / Arabic perspective it offers on Western history and culture. Mr. Shah also covers the influence of the Sufi in Asia to lesser extent. It’s a perspective I haven’t been exposed to before and I find it fascinating.<br /><br />Mr. Shah works very hard to trace Sufic roots for many Western cultural phenomena, schools of thought, and traditions. This book was written at a time when Western historians were only beginning to acknowledge the influence that Arab, Persian, and Sufi culture had on the Western world.<br /><br />Reading this work today, we have a far greater appreciation of the profound influence that Middle Eastern culture had on Europe during the Middle Ages. We know now that many essential aspects of Medieval Europe—from math and science, to philosophy and art, to religious ritual and orders—were borrowed and adapted from the Arab and Persian regions of the Middle East, either via the Crusades or through Saracen Spain.<br /><br />Even now, it’s likely that we still haven’t plumbed the full extent of this influence. In tracing the Sufic roots and influences of various aspects of Western culture, Mr. Shah offers a wealth of compelling evidence to expand our sense of just how much the Western world owes to Arabic, Persian, and Sufi culture and thought. His chapter, “Mysteries in the West: V. The Secret Doctrine” offers a particularly compelling take on the relationship between the Arab East and Christian West during the Middle Ages and after.<br /><br />However, the benefits of reading <i>The Sufis</i> weren’t easy for me to get to. I encountered some major stumbling blocks in the experience of reading this book.<br /><br />One is that fact that it isn’t organized in any way that I’m familiar with for a work whose purpose is to explore the nature and history of a spiritual belief system.<br /><br />I expected something along the lines of a philosophical discourse or a chronological history. But <i>The Sufis</i> isn’t either. While there are many names and dates mentioned throughout, it’s not a chronological tale of the Sufi and the diffusion of Sufi ideas. It's also not a pure philosophical or theological treatise. Given what this book teaches us about the Sufi, it can't be either of those things. Neither would convey a correct vision of the Sufi perspective.<br /><br />This book is essentially a series of snapshots of the Sufi world—short biographies of some important figures; brief explorations of some central ideas; some tracings of the influence that the Sufi have had on history. As Mr. Shah states on p. 278, “We have various aspects of this diffusion in this book, chosen in order to illustrate it, not as any complete ennunciation.”<br /><br />Another stumbling block is the language of the work. There’s an obfuscation to the text beyond it’s dense, scholarly tone. The scholarly tone is a product of its time—the work was published in 1964 for an academic press. Academia in 1964 had a certain style and this density is to be expected. The obfuscation comes from a deeper Sufic paradigm. The Sufi have a way of seeing the world that doesn’t translate fully into normal language patterns and expressions. Everything in this book needs to be read as more than merely information and description—this content is also allegorical and metaphorical. But these allegories and metaphors are created from a pool of cultural reference that’s largely unfamiliar to me.<br /><br />I know that I can’t understand everything Mr. Shah meant to convey. I believe it’s a beneficial effort to try, and to understand what I can as well as I can, but it’s disappointing to know that there’s meaning in this work that’s inaccessible to me.<br /><br />The biggest stumbling block in this book for me, however, is this:<br /><br />At the very beginning of <i>The Sufis</i>, Mr. Shah warns the reader that Sufism isn’t something that can be explained in words. It’s not something that anyone can understand just by reading about it. Sufism must be experienced, and the experience must be guided by a qualified teacher. He emphasizes this point repeatedly throughout the book. As he states on p. 180, “[T]hings which are comprehensive experiences ‘cannot be penned by a mumbling wordsmith, any more than he himself would accept a paper copy of a fruit as edible or nutritious.’” It therefore begs the question:<br /><br />If Sufism can’t be explained by writing about it, why did he bother writing a 450 page book on the subject?<br /><br />More to the point, why should I bother to read it?<br /><br />This alone makes the work very difficult for me to commit to. But my misgivings on this front go even deeper.<br /><br />Several times throughout this book, Mr. Shah talks about the unique teaching methods of the Sufi. Greatest stress is placed on the belief that proper guidance along the Sufi path requires that the seeker be exposed to the proper experiences at the proper time and in the proper context. Only at the correct time and in the correct context can the correct experience lead to the correct psychological development.<br /><br />For a seeker to attempt an act, to hear a poem or a song or a story, to be exposed to an experience at the wrong time, in the wrong context—when the seeker isn’t prepared to experience and understand it correctly—will not merely fail to render to proper outcome: it can actively impede the seeker’s forward progress by promoting an incorrect mindset which will perpetuate further misunderstanding.<br /><br />Taken in these terms, I can’t help but wonder if this entire book—written for a non-Sufi, Western audience—is the wrong lessons, communicated to the wrong people, at the wrong time, with insufficient control to ensure the proper context. Given Mr. Shah's own belief system, this whole endeavor comes across as irresponsible.<br /><br />Thus, we return to my original question:<br /><br />Does <i>The Sufis</i> successfully convey an understanding of the Sufi to a non-Sufi reader?<br /><br />Yes. To an unavoidably limited extent.<br /><br />But if you’re really interested in the Sufi, you’re probably better off reading Sufi works than reading a work about the Sufi.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11594035583905079421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Indeed, during times of crisis, he has been expected to appear on television and dispense political wisdom. \n\nMost often, however, he stays out of the fray and opts for a supreme sense of empathy to guide his words. As excavator--of earth, of his beloved Gaelic, of his own life--Heaney is unmatched. In &quot;Bone Dreams&quot;, the archaeologist\'s task is synonymous with reaching for a cultural past: I push back through dictions, Elizabethan canopies, Norman devices, the erotic mayflowers of Provence and the ivied Latins of churchmen to the scop\'s twang, the iron flash of consonants cleaving the line.\n\nAnd in early poems like &quot;Blackberry Picking&quot;, Heaney\'s images--deftly, delightfully--carry us back to childhood fields: At first, just one, a glossy purple clot Among others, red, green, hard as a knot. You ate that first one and its flesh was sweet Like thickened wine: summer\'s blood was in it Leaving stains upon the tongue and lust for Picking. Then red ones inked up and that hunger Sent us out with milk cans, pea tins, jam pots Where briars scratched and wet grass bleached our boots. Round hayfields, cornfields and potato drills We trekked and picked until the cans were full... Opened Ground is a pleasure and a triumph.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/32851.Idries_Shah">About Idries Shah</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/32851.Idries_Shah"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1518192418p3/32851.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/32851.Idries_Shah">Idries Shah

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12478109517747435141">Idries Shah (Persian: ادریس شاه), also known as Idris Shah, né Sayed Idries el-Hashimi (Arabic: سيد إدريس هاشمي), was an author and teacher in the Sufi tradition who wrote over three dozen critically acclaimed books on topics ranging from psychology and spirituality to travelogues and culture studies.<br/><br/>Born in India, the descendant of a family of Afghan nobles, Shah grew up mainly in England. His ea</span>
  <span id="freeText12478109517747435141" style="display:none">Idries Shah (Persian: ادریس شاه), also known as Idris Shah, né Sayed Idries el-Hashimi (Arabic: سيد إدريس هاشمي), was an author and teacher in the Sufi tradition who wrote over three dozen critically acclaimed books on topics ranging from psychology and spirituality to travelogues and culture studies.<br /><br />Born in India, the descendant of a family of Afghan nobles, Shah grew up mainly in England. His early writings centred on magic and witchcraft. In 1960 he established a publishing house, Octagon Press, producing translations of Sufi classics as well as titles of his own. His most seminal work was The Sufis, which appeared in 1964 and was well received internationally. In 1965, Shah founded the Institute for Cultural Research, a London-based educational charity devoted to the study of human behaviour and culture. A similar organisation, the Institute for the Study of Human Knowledge (ISHK), exists in the United States, under the directorship of Stanford University psychology professor Robert Ornstein, whom Shah appointed as his deputy in the U.S.<br /><br />In his writings, Shah presented Sufism as a universal form of wisdom that predated Islam. Emphasising that Sufism was not static but always adapted itself to the current time, place and people, he framed his teaching in Western psychological terms. Shah made extensive use of traditional teaching stories and parables, texts that contained multiple layers of meaning designed to trigger insight and self-reflection in the reader. He is perhaps best known for his collections of humorous Mulla Nasrudin stories.<br /><br />Shah was at times criticised by orientalists who questioned his credentials and background. His role in the controversy surrounding a new translation of the Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam, published by his friend Robert Graves and his older brother Omar Ali-Shah, came in for particular scrutiny. However, he also had many notable defenders, chief among them the novelist Doris Lessing. Shah came to be recognised as a spokesman for Sufism in the West and lectured as a visiting professor at a number of Western universities. His works have played a significant part in presenting Sufism as a secular, individualistic form of spiritual wisdom.<br /><br />Idries Shah's books on Sufism achieved considerable critical acclaim. He was the subject of a BBC documentary ("One Pair of Eyes") in 1969, and two of his works (The Way of the Sufi and Reflections) were chosen as "Outstanding Book of the Year" by the BBC's "The Critics" programme. Among other honours, Shah won six first prizes at the UNESCO World Book Year in 1973, and the Islamic scholar James Kritzeck, commenting on Shah's Tales of the Dervishes, said that it was "beautifully translated".<br />The reception of Shah's movement was also marked by much controversy. Some orientalists were hostile, in part because Shah presented classical Sufi writings as tools for self-development to be used by contemporary people, rather than as objects of historical study. L. P. Elwell-Sutton from Edinburgh University, Shah's fiercest critic, described his books as "trivial", replete with errors of fact, slovenly and inaccurate translations and even misspellings of Oriental names and words – "a muddle of platitudes, irrelevancies and plain mumbo-jumbo", adding for good measure that Shah had "a remarkable opinion of his own importance". Expressing amusement and amazement at the "sycophantic manner" of Shah's interlocutors in a BBC radio interview, Elwell-Sutton concluded that some Western intellectuals were "so desperate to find answers to the questions that baffle them, that, confronted with wisdom from 'the mysterious East,' they abandon their critical faculties and submit to brainwashing of the crudest kind". To Elwell-Sutton, Shah's Sufism belonged to the realm of "Pseudo-Sufism", "centred not on God but on man."<br /><br />Doris Lessing, one of Shah's greatest defenders,stated in a 1981 interview: "I found Sufism as taught by Idries Shah, which claim</span>
  <a data-text-id="12478109517747435141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
